<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome sequence and analysis of the Japanese morning glory Ipomoea nil"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2016 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2016-11-08"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2016 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Ipomoea is the largest genus in the family Convolvulaceae. Ipomoea nil (Japanese morning glory) has been utilized as a model plant to study the genetic basis of floricultural traits, with over 1,500 mutant lines. In the present study, we have utilized second- and third-generation-sequencing platforms, and have reported a draft genome of I. nil with a scaffold N50 of 2.88&#8201;Mb (contig N50 of 1.87&#8201;Mb), covering 98% of the 750&#8201;Mb genome. Scaffolds covering 91.42% of the assembly are anchored to 15 pseudo-chromosomes. The draft genome has enabled the identification and cataloguing of the Tpn1 family transposons, known as the major mutagen of I. nil, and analysing the dwarf gene, CONTRACTED, located on the genetic map published in 1956. Comparative genomics has suggested that a whole genome duplication in Convolvulaceae, distinct from the recent Solanaceae event, has occurred after the divergence of the two sister families. Japanese morning glory (Ipomoea nil) has diverse flowering traits. Here, the authors describe the reference genome sequence of I. nil, annotations of genes and transposons, and compare evolution of the I. nilgenome to other Convolvulaceae and Solanales genomes.
                  
                    
                  
                "/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Ipomoea</i> is the largest genus in the family Convolvulaceae. <i>Ipomoea nil</i> (Japanese morning glory) has been utilized as a model plant to study the genetic basis of floricultural traits, with over 1,500 mutant lines. In the present study, we have utilized second- and third-generation-sequencing platforms, and have reported a draft genome of <i>I. nil</i> with a scaffold N50 of 2.88âMb (contig N50 of 1.87âMb), covering 98% of the 750âMb genome. Scaffolds covering 91.42% of the assembly are anchored to 15 pseudo-chromosomes. The draft genome has enabled the identification and cataloguing of the <i>Tpn1</i> family transposons, known as the major mutagen of <i>I. nil</i>, and analysing the dwarf gene, <i>CONTRACTED</i>, located on the genetic map published in 1956. Comparative genomics has suggested that a whole genome duplication in Convolvulaceae, distinct from the recent Solanaceae event, has occurred after the divergence of the two sister families.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The genus <i>Ipomoea</i>, which includes 600â700 monophyletic species, is the largest genus in the family Convolvulaceae and is a sister group to the family Solanaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Austin, D. D. F. &amp; HuÃ¡man, Z. A synopsis of Ipomoea (Convolvulaceae) in the Americas. Taxon 45, 3â38 (1996)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1196">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Stefanovic, S., Krueger, L. &amp; Olmstead, R. G. Monophyly of the Convolvulaceae and circumscription of their major lineages based on DNA sequences of multiple chloroplast loci. Am. J. Bot. 89, 1510â1522 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1199">2</a></sup>. These species exhibit various flower morphologies and pigmentation patterns<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Clegg, M. T. &amp; Durbin, M. L. Tracing floral adaptations from ecology to molecules. Nat. Rev. Genet. 4, 206â215 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1203">3</a></sup>, and are distributed worldwide<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Austin, D. D. F. &amp; HuÃ¡man, Z. A synopsis of Ipomoea (Convolvulaceae) in the Americas. Taxon 45, 3â38 (1996)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1207">1</a></sup>. Morning glory species, including <i>Ipomoea nil, Ipomoea purpurea, Ipomoea tricolor</i> and <i>Ipomoea batatas</i> (sweet potato), are commercially important species. Japanese morning glory (<i>I. nil</i>), locally known as Asagao, is a climbing annual herb producing blue flowers capable of self-pollination (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 1aâl</a>). It is believed to have been introduced from China to Japan in the eighth century, and has become a traditional floricultural plant in Japan since the seventeenth century. Most of Japanese elementary students grow it, as part of their school curriculum. The genetics of <i>I. nil</i> has been extensively studied for more than 100 years, and it has been a model plant for the study of photoperiodic flowering and flower colouration. A number of spontaneous mutants of <i>I. nil</i> have been identified since the early nineteenth century. Most of their mutations were related to floricultural traits, and several variants with combinations of mutations have been developed (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 1mâaa</a>). The unique features of <i>I. nil</i>, for example, blue flowers and vine movements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Fukada-Tanaka, S., Inagaki, Y., Yamaguchi, T., Saito, N. &amp; Iida, S. Colour-enhancing protein in blue petals. Nature 407, 581 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1237">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Kitazawa, D. et al. Shoot circumnutation and winding movements require gravisensing cells. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 18742â18747 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1240">5</a></sup>, have been characterized by using the cultivars carrying such mutations.</p><p>More than 1,500 cultivars of <i>I. nil</i> are maintained by the Stock Center at Kyushu University as a part of the National BioResource Project. Our recent studies have revealed that many of these mutant lines have been the result of mutagenic activity by <i>Tpn1</i> family transposons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Fukada-Tanaka, S., Inagaki, Y., Yamaguchi, T., Saito, N. &amp; Iida, S. Colour-enhancing protein in blue petals. Nature 407, 581 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1253">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Inagaki, Y., Hisatomi, Y., Suzuki, T., Kasahara, K. &amp; Iida, S. Isolation of a Suppressor-mutator/Enhancer-like transposable element, Tpn1, from Japanese morning glory bearing variegated flowers. Plant Cell 6, 375â383 (1994)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1256">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Hoshino, A., Park, K. I. &amp; Iida, S. Identification of r mutations conferring white flowers in the Japanese morning glory (Ipomoea nil). J. Plant Res. 122, 215â222 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1259">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Morita, Y. et al. A chalcone isomerase-like protein enhances flavonoid production and flower pigmentation. Plant J. 78, 294â304 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1262">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Iwasaki, M. &amp; Nitasaka, E. The FEATHERED gene is required for polarity establishment in lateral organs especially flowers of the Japanese morning glory (Ipomoea nil). Plant Mol. Biol. 62, 913â925 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1265">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Nitasaka, E. Insertion of an En/Spm-related transposable element into a floral homeotic gene DUPLICATED causes a double flower phenotype in the Japanese morning glory. Plant J. 36, 522â531 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1268">10</a></sup>. These transposons are class II elements and members of <i>En/Spm</i> or CACTA superfamily that can transpose via a cut-and-paste mechanism. The maize <i>En/Spm</i> elements encode two transposase genes for TnpA and TnpD, mediating transposition of <i>En/Spm</i> and its derivatives<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Kunze, R. &amp; Weil, C. F. The hAT and CACTA superfamilies of plant transposons. in Mobile DNA II eds Craig N. L., Craigie R., Gellert M., Lambowitz A. M. 565â610ASM Press (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1282">11</a></sup>. TnpA and TnpD bind to the sub-terminal repetitive regions (SRRs) and terminal inverted repeats (TIRs) of <i>En/Spm</i>, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Kunze, R. &amp; Weil, C. F. The hAT and CACTA superfamilies of plant transposons. in Mobile DNA II eds Craig N. L., Craigie R., Gellert M., Lambowitz A. M. 565â610ASM Press (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1289">11</a></sup>. The copy number of the <i>Tpn1</i> family was estimated to be 500â1,000, and almost 40 copies have been characterized<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Fukada-Tanaka, S., Inagaki, Y., Yamaguchi, T., Saito, N. &amp; Iida, S. Colour-enhancing protein in blue petals. Nature 407, 581 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1296">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Inagaki, Y., Hisatomi, Y., Suzuki, T., Kasahara, K. &amp; Iida, S. Isolation of a Suppressor-mutator/Enhancer-like transposable element, Tpn1, from Japanese morning glory bearing variegated flowers. Plant Cell 6, 375â383 (1994)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1299">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Hoshino, A., Park, K. I. &amp; Iida, S. Identification of r mutations conferring white flowers in the Japanese morning glory (Ipomoea nil). J. Plant Res. 122, 215â222 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1302">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Morita, Y. et al. A chalcone isomerase-like protein enhances flavonoid production and flower pigmentation. Plant J. 78, 294â304 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1305">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Iwasaki, M. &amp; Nitasaka, E. The FEATHERED gene is required for polarity establishment in lateral organs especially flowers of the Japanese morning glory (Ipomoea nil). Plant Mol. Biol. 62, 913â925 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1308">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Nitasaka, E. Insertion of an En/Spm-related transposable element into a floral homeotic gene DUPLICATED causes a double flower phenotype in the Japanese morning glory. Plant J. 36, 522â531 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1311">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Kawasaki, S. &amp; Nitasaka, E. Characterization of Tpn1 family in the Japanese morning glory: En/Spm-related transposable elements capturing host genes. Plant Cell Physiol. 45, 933â944 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1315">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Morita, Y., Ishiguro, K., Tanaka, Y., Iida, S. &amp; Hoshino, A. Spontaneous mutations of the UDP-glucose:flavonoid 3-O-glucosyltransferase gene confers pale- and dull-colored flowers in the Japanese and common morning glories. Planta 242, 575â587 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1318">13</a></sup>. All of the transposons characterized thus far are non-autonomous elements, and no elements encoding intact transposase genes have been identified. The non-autonomous <i>Tpn1</i> family elements have a characteristic structure and are known to capture genic regions from the host genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Kawasaki, S. &amp; Nitasaka, E. Characterization of Tpn1 family in the Japanese morning glory: En/Spm-related transposable elements capturing host genes. Plant Cell Physiol. 45, 933â944 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1326">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Takahashi, S., Inagaki, Y., Satoh, H., Hoshino, A. &amp; Iida, S. Capture of a genomic HMG domain sequence by the En/Spm-related transposable element Tpn1 in the Japanese morning glory. Mol. Gen. Genet. 261, 447â451 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1329">14</a></sup>. Their internal sequences are substituted with the captured host sequences, whereas their terminal regions necessary for transposition are conserved. Some of the internal genic regions are transcribed; a <i>Tpn1</i> transposon integrated in the <i>DFR-B</i> gene for anthocyanin pigment biosynthesis generates chimeric transcripts consisting of both the <i>DFR-B</i> and the captured intragenic region<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Takahashi, S., Inagaki, Y., Satoh, H., Hoshino, A. &amp; Iida, S. Capture of a genomic HMG domain sequence by the En/Spm-related transposable element Tpn1 in the Japanese morning glory. Mol. Gen. Genet. 261, 447â451 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1342">14</a></sup>.</p><p><i>I. nil</i> has 15 pairs of chromosomes (2<i>n</i>=30)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Yasui, K. Studies on Pharbitis nil Chois. II Chromosome number. Bot. Mag. Tokyo 42, 480â485 (1928)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1354">15</a></sup>. However, the original classical map from 1956 contained only ten linkage groups, as a result of mapping 71 genetic loci out of 219 analysed loci to one of the ten linkage groups<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Hagiwara, T. Genes and chromosome maps in the Japanese morning glory. Bull. Res. Coll. Agr. Vet. Sci. Nihon Univ. 5, 34â56 (1956)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1358">16</a></sup>. The genetic information of <i>I. nil</i> available to date includes the linkage map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Hagiwara, T. Genes and chromosome maps in the Japanese morning glory. Bull. Res. Coll. Agr. Vet. Sci. Nihon Univ. 5, 34â56 (1956)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1365">16</a></sup>, 62,300 expressed sequence tags (ESTs) deposited to the DDBJ/EMBL/NCBI databases, simple sequence repeat markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Ly, T. et al. Development of EST-SSR markers of Ipomoea nil. Breed. Sci. 62, 99â104 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1370">17</a></sup> and a recent large-scale transcriptome assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Wei, C. et al. De novo transcriptome assembly of Ipomoea nil using Illumina sequencing for gene discovery and SSR marker identification. Mol. Genet. Genomics 290, 1873â1884 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1374">18</a></sup>. The availability of a reference genome sequence would give researchers a standard with which to compare their mutant lines and would fast track genomic analysis of mutations. The genome of a closely related species of a wild sweet potato, <i>Ipomoea trifida</i>, was recently sequenced and published<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Hirakawa, H. et al. Survey of genome sequences in a wild sweet potato, Ipomoea trifida (H. B. K.) G. Don. DNA Res. 22, 171â179 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1381">19</a></sup>, in which they reported genome sequences of two <i>I. trifida</i> lines analysed using Illumina HiSeq platform, with average scaffold lengths of 6.6âkb (N50=43âkb) and 3.9âkb (N50=36âkb), respectively. However, the assembled scaffolds did not have chromosomal level information, and were highly fragmented.</p><p>In the present study, we report a pseudo-chromosomal level whole genome assembly of a wild-type <i>I. nil</i> line, with an estimated genome size of 750âMb, sequenced using PacBioâs Single Molecule, Real-Time Technology (SMRT) and Illumina sequencers. We have also identified two copies of <i>Tpn1</i> family transposons encoding putative TnpA and TnpD transposases, 339 other non-autonomous <i>Tpn1</i> transposon copies, as well as the most likely candidate for the dwarf gene, <i>CONTRACTED</i>, mapped on the classical genetic map.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">DNA sequencing and genome assembly</h3><p>One individual plant of the wild-type line, Tokyo Kokei Standard (TKS), was used for genome sequencing. Its genome size was estimated to be approximately 750âMb using flow cytometry (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). PacBio sequencing yielded 5.74 million reads (39.4âGB, 52.6 Ã coverage and N50 of 10.3âkb), with the longest and the average read lengths being 48.1 and 6.8âkb respectively, whereas, sequencing using the Illumina HiSeq (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 1</a>) included two short and six long insert libraries. With an initial read length of 150âbp, the short reads covered approximately 906 Ã of the genome. The work-flow for the PacBio data assembly consisted of seven steps (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). Initial <i>de novo</i> assembly of the PacBio reads resulted in 736.4âMb of genome assembly, with a contig N50 of 1.83âMb. To remove left-over residual errors originating from PacBio sequences, the short reads from Illumina were aligned against the assembled genome to identify homozygous variants. The homozygous variants amounted to 1,532 SNPs, 20,479 deletions and 6,549 insertions showing that the assembly had 99.99% base accuracy. The insertion-deletion (in-del) errors had outnumbered the substitution errors, similar to the results observed in PacBio-based <i>Vigna angularis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Sakai, H. et al. The power of single molecule real-time sequencing technology in the de novo assembly of a eukaryotic genome. Sci. Rep. 5, 16780 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1429">20</a></sup> and <i>Oropetium thomaeum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="VanBuren, R. et al. Single-molecule sequencing of the desiccation-tolerant grass Oropetium thomaeum. Nature 527, 508â511 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1436">21</a></sup> genome assemblies, and were replaced with the Illumina sequence bases. Mitochondrial and chloroplast derived sequences were identified to be 1.15âMb from 51 contigs and were removed. The organellar genomes were sequenced using a Sanger sequencer and assembled separately (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Methods</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Figs 4 and 5</a>). Scaffolding using Illumina longer range mate-pair libraries and subsequent gap-filling using PacBio reads increased the N50 to 3.72âMb. The assembly statistics at each step of the work-flow are mentioned in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 2</a>. An independent assembly of Illumina reads using SOAPdenovo2 assembler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1449">22</a></sup> resulted in 1.1âGb of genome assembly. The assembly size was reduced to 768âMb, with a scaffold N50 of 3.5âMb and a contig N50 of 9.5âkb, when considering only contigs and scaffolds longer than 1âkb. The assembly statistics of both the PacBio and Illumina assemblies are compared in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 3</a>. The PacBio version of the assembly was chosen for downstream analysis owing to PacBioâs longer read lengths vastly increasing the contiguity of the assembled genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Mis-assembly detection and pseudo-molecule construction</h3><p>Illumina sequencing employing the RAD-seq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Baird, N. A. et al. Rapid SNP discovery and genetic mapping using sequenced RAD markers. PLoS ONE 3, e3376 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1465">23</a></sup> procedure, yielded 86.1 million reads for the parent samples and 562.2 million reads for the progeny samples (read length of 150âbp). Filtering the SNP markers obtained using the STACKS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Catchen, J. M. et al. Stacks: building and genotyping loci de novo from short-read sequences. G3 1, 171â182 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1469">24</a></sup> pipeline resulted in 3,733 SNP markers from 176 samples. Fifteen linkage maps (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>) were constructed using the SNP markers and were helpful in identifying inconsistent scaffolds which were present in more than one linkage group. To eliminate the possibility of mis-assembled chimeric scaffolds, the scaffolds were split at their junction points into two separate scaffolds using the linkage maps as a reference. In the case of mis-assemblies at the contig level, each chimeric region was split into three parts such that the first and the last part would belong to two different chromosomes from the linkage map, whereas the middle part would still remain chimeric, albeit with a shorter length (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). A first splitting procedure was employed to split 52 scaffolds, after the scaffolding phase of the assembly process. After gap-filling, another splitting procedure was used to break 29 additional scaffolds. The major achievement of the assembly procedure was that, even after splitting chimeric scaffolds, the N50 values obtained for scaffolds and contigs were still 2.88 and 1.87âMb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Tab1">Table 1</a>), respectively, which is comparable to assemblies achieved utilizing traditional Sanger sequencing data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Michael, T. P. &amp; Jackson, S. The first 50 plant genomes. Plant Genome 6, 1â7 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1483">25</a></sup>. The mapping of scaffolds to linkage maps not only aided in identifying potential mis-assemblies, but also guided the generation of pseudo-chromosomes from the available scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Data 1</a>). The pseudo-chromosomes accounted for 91.42% of the assembly (N50 of 44.78âMb), along with unoriented scaffolds (around 25.53% of the assembly), and are represented in a circular display, with predicted genomic features along the 15 pseudo-chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig1">Fig. 1aâf</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 <i>I. nil</i> genome assembly statistics.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ncomms13295/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Genomic characterizations of I. nil."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Genomic characterizations of <i>I. nil</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms13295/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms13295/MediaObjects/41467_2016_Article_BFncomms13295_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms13295/MediaObjects/41467_2016_Article_BFncomms13295_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="677" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Outer circle displaying the 15 pseudo-chromosomes in 1âMb units. <i>TpnA2</i>â<i>4</i> (blue dashes) and putative centromeric locations (black dashes) are also denoted in the outer circle. (<b>b</b>) Location of <i>Tpn1</i> family transposons. (<b>c</b>) Gene density per Mb. (<b>d</b>) Coverage of copia (magenta) and gypsy (turquoise) LTRs per Mb. (<b>e</b>) Repeat coverage per Mb. (<b>f</b>) Syntenic regions containing more than 10 paralogous genes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms13295/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Assembly validation</h3><p>The Core Eukaryotic Genes Mapping Approach, or CEGMA pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061â1067 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1844">26</a></sup> and more recently, the BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1848">27</a></sup> pipeline have become commonly used protocols to validate the completeness of assembly projects by examination of coverage of highly conserved genes. The percentage of completeness for our assembly was 94.35 and 99.60% for completely and partially aligned core eukaryotic genes, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 4</a>). BUSCO analysis revealed a completeness score of around 95% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 5</a>). This indicated that most of the evolutionarily conserved core gene set was present in the <i>I. nil</i> assembly suggesting a high quality assembly. To further validate the assembly, the newly generated <i>I. nil</i> ESTs, BAC-end and RNA-seq data were utilized. Comparisons against 93,691 ESTs showed that 99.11% of them were aligned, with 97.40% of the ESTs having at least 90% of their lengths covered in the alignments. Using 20,847 BAC-end read pairs, it was found that 94.92% of the reads were paired in the same scaffold with a mean insert length near the 100âkb mark (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>), and 97.87% of the reads were paired in the same pseudo-chromosome. RNA-seq reads from six different tissues including leaf, flower, embryo, stem, root and seed coat tissues, when aligned against the assembled sequence, showed that around 94.7 and 96% of the read pairs were aligned in the embryo sample and the remaining five samples, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 6</a>). The high quality of the assembly verified by CEGMA and BUSCO was corroborated by the ESTs and BAC-end sequences. Five whole BAC sequences (approximately 100âkb in length) were also completely covered in the scaffolds with minor in-dels (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 7</a>). One of the BAC sequences included 12.6âkb of the <i>Tpn1</i> family transposon, <i>TpnA2</i> (see below), suggesting that repetitive elements with high copy numbers and relatively long sequences were successfully determined. The SOAPdenovo assembly was also able to cover the five BAC sequences, but with large in-dels and an increased number of mismatches, indicating that per-base resolution was better in the assembly using PacBio reads. Telomeric repeats, centromeric repeats, and rDNA arrays were identified to further analyse the contiguity of the assembly. Thirty scaffolds, with telomeric repeat units (AAACCCT) in the range of 47.1 to 4,613.9 repeating units, were identified, of which 13 were completely covered by the tandem repeats and could not be incorporated into the linkage maps (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 8</a>). Pseudo-chromosomes 2, 6, 8 and 14 were found to have telomeric repeats at both the ends, while pseudo-chromosomes 3, 4, 5, 9, 10, 12, 13 and 15 had telomeric repeats at only one end. Although SOAPdenovo assembly captured 27 telomeric repeat sequences, the average size of the repeats was five times longer in the PacBio assembly. The ribosomal DNAs (rDNAs) in the order of 18S, 5.8S and 25S rDNAs are found to occur in tandem arrays typically spanning several megabase pairs in regions called nucleolar organizer regions (NORs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="VanBuren, R. et al. Single-molecule sequencing of the desiccation-tolerant grass Oropetium thomaeum. Nature 527, 508â511 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1884">21</a></sup>. Three scaffolds were found to contain 3 NOR units and 34 scaffolds had 2 NOR units (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 9</a>). In total, 1,212 5S rDNA sequences were clustered in 21 scaffolds that were located away from the scaffolds carrying NORs. Centromeric repeats are known to span hundreds of kilobase pairs to several megabase pairs and are difficult to be assembled owing to their repetitive complexity. The centromeric monomer sequence was identified to be 173âbp in length (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). Using the monomeric sequence as a base, the longest centromeric repeat stretches were identified for each chromosome and the analysis revealed that two of the identified centromeric repeat stretches were longer than 100âkb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 10</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Repeat analysis and identification of <i>Tpn1</i> transposons</h3><p>Analysis using RepeatModeler (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 11</a>) showed that LTRs (long terminal repeats) comprised the largest portion of predicted repeats. The unclassified elements were mined for copia and gypsy repeats using RepBase. Copia and gypsy elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 12</a>) comprised 12.92 and 14.46% of the assembled genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig1">Fig. 1d</a>). DNA class repeat elements represented 5.60% of the genome. Altogether, 63.29% of the genome was predicted to be repetitive (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig1">Fig. 1e</a>). However, RepeatModeler was not able to predict <i>Tpn1</i> family transposons (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). Hence, an in-house pipeline based on the presence of 5â² and 3â² TIRs as well as target site duplications (TSDs) was used to identify the <i>Tpn1</i> transposons. In total, 339 <i>Tpn1</i> transposons were identified with an average length of 7,081âbp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Data 2</a>). The smallest identified was 161âbp in length, while the longest was 40,619âbp. All the transposons had 3-bp TSDs, with the exception of one that had a 5-bp TSD (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 13</a>). Fourteen of them had a mismatch in their TSDs. The TSDs tended to be AT rich (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>), with at least one of A or T bp appearing in 95% of the TSDs. A nucleotide BLAST analysis revealed that most of the <i>Tpn1</i> transposons carried SRR sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>) in both 5â² and 3â² terminal regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Data 2</a>). Because TIR and SRR sequences are <i>cis</i>-requirements for transposition, it can be suggested that the <i>Tpn1</i> transposons are capable of transposition. However, thirty-two of the identified <i>Tpn1</i> transposons contained large rearrangements in SRR indicating that they are inactive. Twenty-nine <i>Tpn1</i> transposons were found within the 5â² UTR and introns of the predicted genes, which could disrupt the function of those genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 14</a>). It could be expected that the autonomous <i>Tpn1</i> family transposons carry both the TnpA and TnpD transposase coding sequences such as <i>En/Spm</i> and related autonomous transposons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Kunze, R. &amp; Weil, C. F. The hAT and CACTA superfamilies of plant transposons. in Mobile DNA II eds Craig N. L., Craigie R., Gellert M., Lambowitz A. M. 565â610ASM Press (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1978">11</a></sup>. A translated BLAST search against the 339 <i>Tpn1</i> family transposons, using TnpA and TnpD sequences from maize and snapdragon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Nacken, W. F., Piotrowiak, R., Saedler, H. &amp; Sommer, H. The transposable element Tam1 from Antirrhinum majus shows structural homology to the maize transposon En/Spm and has no sequence specificity of insertion. Mol. Gen. Genet. 228, 201â208 (1991)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d3400e1986">28</a></sup> as queries, revealed that two transposons, named <i>TpnA3</i> and <i>TpnA4</i>, carried <i>TnpD</i> homologues, with two copies of <i>TpnA3</i> residing in the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). No obvious <i>TnpA</i> homologues were identified in the 339 transposons. Also, no transcripts corresponding to <i>TnpA</i> and <i>TnpD</i> were found in the predicted genes or transcripts, indicating transcription of the transposase sequences was silenced in the line TKS. To identify autonomous transposons, the cDNA fragments for <i>TnpA</i> and <i>TnpD</i> homologues were isolated by a series of RTâPCRs from the line Q1072, where <i>Tpn1</i> actively transposes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 1m</a>). A nucleotide BLAST search, against the whole-genome sequence using the isolated cDNA sequences as queries, identified two transposons with <i>TnpA</i> and <i>TnpD</i> sequences, designated as <i>TpnA1</i> and <i>TpnA2</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). Of these, <i>TpnA2</i> is truncated in the genome, while the 5â² terminus of <i>TpnA1</i> was not completely captured in the draft genome assembly. To characterize the entire <i>TpnA1</i> sequence, a BAC clone from TKS carrying <i>TpnA1</i> was isolated and sequenced. <i>TpnA1</i> is the putative autonomous element, because it carries apparently functional TIR and SRR sequences, in addition to the coding sequences of TnpA and TnpD. No transposons carrying TnpA coding sequences alone were found. In total, the genome contained two TnpA and five TnpD putative coding sequence copies (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). The deduced amino acid sequences of the transposases were highly conserved in the genome and shared conserved domains with known transposases of <i>En/Spm</i> and snapdragon <i>Tam1</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Nacken, W. F., Piotrowiak, R., Saedler, H. &amp; Sommer, H. The transposable element Tam1 from Antirrhinum majus shows structural homology to the maize transposon En/Spm and has no sequence specificity of insertion. Mol. Gen. Genet. 228, 201â208 (1991)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2068">28</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="The Tpn1 family transposons encoding transposases."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: The <i>Tpn1</i> family transposons encoding transposases.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms13295/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms13295/MediaObjects/41467_2016_Article_BFncomms13295_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms13295/MediaObjects/41467_2016_Article_BFncomms13295_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="222" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The orange, yellow and blue boxes indicate transposons, untranslated regions, and coding sequences respectively. The numerals above the blue boxes show exon numbers, and the arrows show the orientations of the transposase genes. The filled triangles are the 122-bp and 104-bp tandem repeats in the 5â² and 3â² sub-terminal regions respectively. <i>TpnA3</i> lacks exons 7 and 8, and <i>TpnA4</i> has a gap represented by a white box, as well as three frame shift mutation indicated by the vertical bar with â1 and +1 in the exon 10 and 13.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms13295/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Gene prediction and functional annotation</h3><p>RNAseq data from leaf, flower, embryo, stem, root and seed coat samples were used to assist in the process of gene prediction. A total of 42,783 gene models were predicted along with 45,365 transcripts, with tomato as the reference species, using Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics 19, ii215âii225 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2114">29</a></sup>. Of the transcripts, 44,916 contained a complete ORF with a start and a stop codon (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 15</a>) and 95.54% of the gene models could be assigned inside the 15 pseudo-chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>). Single exon genes accounted for 17.52% of the total. Two thirds of the transcripts were found to have less than or equal to 5 exons. A total of 61.99% of the gene models were annotated using the UniProt-Swiss-Prot database and in the remaining gene models, 16.93% were annotated using the UniProt-Trembl database. In addition, 61.92% of the gene models were assigned Pfam domain annotations. In total, the combined annotation procedure was able to assign annotations for 79.12% of the gene models (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Data 3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Analysing the dwarf gene of <i>CONTRACTED</i></h3><p>The recessive <i>contracted</i> (<i>ct</i>) mutants show dwarfism with dark-green, thick, and wrinkled leaves and cotyledons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Imai, Y. Linkage groups of the Japanese morning glory. Genetics 14, 223â255 (1929)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2144">30</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). Their flowers and seeds are relatively small (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 1nâp</a>). Mutants carrying mutable <i>ct</i> alleles showing somatic reversion to the wild type were reported in the 1930s; however, existing <i>ct</i> mutants showed no mutable phenotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Imai, Y. On the mutable genes of Pharbitis, with special reference to their bearing on the mechanism of bud-variation. J. Coll. Agr. Tokyo Imp. Univ 12, 479â523 (1934)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2161">31</a></sup>. The <i>ct</i> locus was mapped on LG5 of the classical linkage map and was located 1.2âcM from the <i>A3</i> locus for the anthocyanin biosynthesis gene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Inagaki, Y., Hisatomi, Y., Suzuki, T., Kasahara, K. &amp; Iida, S. Isolation of a Suppressor-mutator/Enhancer-like transposable element, Tpn1, from Japanese morning glory bearing variegated flowers. Plant Cell 6, 375â383 (1994)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2171">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Hagiwara, T. Genes and chromosome maps in the Japanese morning glory. Bull. Res. Coll. Agr. Vet. Sci. Nihon Univ. 5, 34â56 (1956)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2174">16</a></sup>. It can be presumed that the <i>CONTRACTED</i> (<i>CT</i>) gene is a brassinosteroid (BR) biosynthesis gene, because BR application rescued the deficiency of <i>ct</i> and <i>kobito</i> (<i>kbt</i>) double mutants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Suzuki, Y. et al. A dwarf mutant strain of Pharbitis nil, Uzukobito (kobito), has defective brassinosteroid biosynthesis. Plant J. 36, 401â410 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2194">32</a></sup>. The <i>kbt</i> mutation was shown to be allelic to the <i>star</i> (<i>s</i>) mutation by crossing the original <i>kbt</i> strain (Q837) and an <i>s</i> strain (Q721) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 1r,s</a>). The <i>STAR</i> gene is a homologue of <i>Arabidopsis DET2</i>, encoding a BR biosynthesis enzyme (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). To isolate the <i>CT</i> gene, BR-related genes were screened from the scaffold, BDFN01000805, carrying the <i>A3</i> gene. A homologue of <i>Arabidopsis ROT3</i> (also known as <i>CYP90C1</i>) encoding the P450 catalyzing C-23 hydroxylation of the BR precursor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Kim, G. T., Tsukaya, H. &amp; Uchimiya, H. The ROTUNDIFOLIA3 gene of Arabidopsis thaliana encodes a new member of the cytochrome P-450 family that is required for the regulated polar elongation of leaf cells. Genes Dev. 12, 2381â2391 (1998)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2240">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Ohnishi, T. et al. C-23 hydroxylation by Arabidopsis CYP90C1 and CYP90D1 reveals a novel shortcut in brassinosteroid biosynthesis. Plant Cell 18, 3275â3288 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2243">34</a></sup> was found 129âkb away from the <i>A3</i> gene in the scaffold, BDFN01000805, and its assigned gene ID was INIL05g09538 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>). All 19 <i>ct</i> mutants tested carried <i>Tpn1</i> family transposon insertions in the first exon of the gene, while no such insertions were found in any of the 24 lines without dwarfism (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 16</a>). There are three mutant alleles of the gene named <i>ct-1</i>, <i>ct-2</i> and <i>ct-w</i>. The <i>ct-1</i> and <i>ct-w</i> alleles carried the <i>Tpn1</i> family transposons, <i>Tpn14</i> and <i>Tpn15</i>, respectively. The <i>ct-2</i> allele was derived from <i>ct-1</i>, and the 5â² half of <i>Tpn14</i> along with the 36âbp sequences immediately upstream of <i>Tpn14</i> were deleted (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). The transposon insertion strongly suppressed accumulation of intact mRNA of the gene in hypocotyls (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>), whereas mRNA accumulation levels of other genes for BR biosynthesis enzyme were normal in the mutants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 16</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="The CT gene for BR synthesis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: The <i>CT</i> gene for BR synthesis.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms13295/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms13295/MediaObjects/41467_2016_Article_BFncomms13295_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms13295/MediaObjects/41467_2016_Article_BFncomms13295_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1102" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Eight-day-old seedlings of the wild-type plant and the <i>ct</i> mutants. (<b>b</b>) Structure of the <i>CT</i> gene and its mutant allele. Physical map surrounding the gene (upper), genomic structure (middle) and the transposon insertion site (lower) are presented. Blue and purple bars on the physical map indicate the positions of the predicted genes with forward and reverse orientations respectively. Blue and green hexagons indicate the orientations of <i>CT</i> and <i>A3</i> respectively, and RAD indicates the nearest RAD markers. Yellow, blue, and orange boxes are untranslated regions, coding sequences, and transposons respectively. The symbols in the orange boxes are the same as in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>. (<b>c</b>) Expression of <i>CT</i> in the hypocotyls detected by reverse transcription PCR (RTâPCR).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms13295/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Genome evolution</h3><p>Protein sequences from rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Ouyang, S. et al. The TIGR rice genome annotation resource: improvements and new features. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, D883âD887 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2380">35</a></sup> (monocotyledon outgroup), grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2384">36</a></sup>, kiwifruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Huang, S. et al. Draft genome of the kiwifruit Actinidia chinensis. Nat. Commun. 4, 2640 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2388">37</a></sup> (from the Asterid clade), along with Solanales order members tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Tomato sequencing consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â641 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2392">38</a></sup>, potato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Sequencing Consortium, T. P. G. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189â195 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2396">39</a></sup> and capsicum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Kim, S. et al. Genome sequence of the hot pepper provides insights into the evolution of pungency in Capsicum species. Nat. Genet. 46, 270â278 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2401">40</a></sup> were used for gene family clustering using the OrthoMCL pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2405">41</a></sup> to infer phylogenetic relationships. A total of 1,353 single copy orthologs corresponding to the seven species were extracted from the clusters and were filtered to 214 single copy orthologs. Phylogenetic inference using RaxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312â1313 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2409">42</a></sup> reconfirmed the phylogenetic arrangement of <i>I. nil</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 17</a>). BEAST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Bouckaert, R. et al. BEAST 2: a software platform for Bayesian evolutionary analysis. PLoS Comput. Biol. 10, 1â6 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2419">43</a></sup> estimated the divergence of <i>I. nil</i> from the other Solanales members to be around 75.25 Myr ago, which was very close to the estimation from the TTOL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Hedges, S. B., Marin, J., Suleski, M., Paymer, M. &amp; Kumar, S. Tree of life reveals clock-like speciation and diversification. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 832â845 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2427">44</a></sup> database (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>). Also, <i>I. nil</i> was estimated to have separated from kiwifruit approximately 105.8 Myr ago. Divergence time estimates obtained for the other species also corresponded well with the estimations from TTOL database.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Genome evolution."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Genome evolution.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms13295/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms13295/MediaObjects/41467_2016_Article_BFncomms13295_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms13295/MediaObjects/41467_2016_Article_BFncomms13295_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="575" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Divergence time estimation using BEAST. The scale bar 20.0 corresponds to Myr ago. The node labels indicate estimated divergence times in Myr ago, with estimations from TTOL in parentheses, and the branch labels indicate the clades within the branch. (<b>b</b>) Distribution of Ks values against the corresponding percentage of syntenic genes, comparing <i>I. nil</i> and tomato against kiwifruit. The colours violet, magenta, orange, turquoise, blue, and purple represent the Ks values of <i>I. nil</i> versus <i>I. nil</i>, <i>S. lycopersicum</i> versus <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>I. nil</i> versus <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>A. chinensis</i> versus <i>A. chinensis</i>, <i>S. lycopersicum</i> versus <i>A. chinensis</i>, and <i>I. nil</i> versus <i>A. chinensis</i> respectively. Speciation events among the three species and lineage specific WGDs are highlighted with arrows.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms13295/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Synteny analysis using MCScanX revealed that 2,275 syntenic gene blocks were found to contain 17,376 paralogous gene pairs in the assembled pseudo-chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig1">Fig. 1f</a>). The number of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (Ks) of the gene pairs in the syntenic regions was plotted against the percentage of corresponding genes to infer and compare whole-genome duplication (WGD) events in <i>I. nil</i>. <i>I. nil</i> and tomato were found to share 47.05% of syntenic orthologs in a 1:1 ratio, whereas, the percentage of kiwifruit orthologs in a 1:1 ratio across tomato and <i>I. nil</i> were 34.89 and 36.01%, respectively. Apart from the 1:1 orthologs, both tomato and <i>I. nil</i> shared large numbers of syntenic blocks with kiwifruit, possibly because of the two recent duplication events in kiwifruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Huang, S. et al. Draft genome of the kiwifruit Actinidia chinensis. Nat. Commun. 4, 2640 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2523">37</a></sup>, which was also evident from the two Ks peaks specific to kiwifruit (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>). A recent WGD event was estimated to have occurred in Solanaceae members, approximately 71Â±19.4 Myr ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Tomato sequencing consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â641 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2530">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Sequencing Consortium, T. P. G. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189â195 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2533">39</a></sup>. A Ks peak from syntenic paralogs of tomato, corresponding to the above mentioned WGD event, was found to occur after the speciation peak between tomato and <i>I. nil</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>), suggesting that the event was specific to the Solanaceae and should have occurred reasonably close, following the divergence which was estimated to be 75.25 Myr ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>). The analysis also revealed a Ks peak specific to <i>I. nil</i> indicating that a WGD event had also occurred, independently, in the Convolvulaceae family (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>).</p><p>Gene family clustering showed that 10,549 core gene families were shared by all four species of the Solanales members (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 18</a>). <i>I. nil</i> contained 2,242 unique gene families not shared by Solanaceae members, whereas the Solanaceae members shared 2,681 more gene families than <i>I. nil</i>. <i>I. nil</i> specific gene families had expansions of paralogs (mean value of 4.92) compared with gene families which had orthologous relationships with the other Solanales (mean value of 1.79). <i>I. nil</i> specific gene families were found to be enriched with pollination and reproductive process related gene ontology (GO) terms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 17</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>The advent of second- and third-generation sequencers have fast-tracked genome assemblies of a variety of species. The current study has utilized nearly the complete potential of recent sequencing tools and has culminated in a highly contiguous genome assembly of <i>I. nil</i>. A few of the recent genome assembly projects have used PacBio data to supplement Illumina based contig assemblies, and a mild improvement in the lengths of the assembled scaffolds have been observed (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 18</a>). However, in this study, PacBio data were used as a base to construct contig assemblies, while Illumina data were used to supplement the assembly, resulting in a marked increase in the lengths of the assemblies observed (scaffold N50 length of 2.88âMb). The average contig N50 length for all published genomes is 50âkb (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="VanBuren, R. et al. Single-molecule sequencing of the desiccation-tolerant grass Oropetium thomaeum. Nature 527, 508â511 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2589">21</a>), whereas <i>I. nil</i> had a contig N50 length of 1.87âMb. The 7-kb size selected inserts of the PacBio sequence data was especially helpful in resolving <i>Tpn1</i> transposons, whose average length was approximately 7âkb, and the assembly also revealed complex repeats like telomeric repeats, rDNA clusters, and centromeric repeats. However, a better resolution of such repeats was obtained in <i>Oropetium thomaeum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="VanBuren, R. et al. Single-molecule sequencing of the desiccation-tolerant grass Oropetium thomaeum. Nature 527, 508â511 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2602">21</a></sup> genome assembly, possibly owing to the 15-kb lower end insert size selection, explaining the importance of longer read lengths in obtaining near-perfect assemblies. The potential of PacBio sequence data in long, eukaryotic genomes has been further showcased in the draft genomes of <i>Gorilla gorilla</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Gordon, D. et al. Long-read sequence assembly of the gorilla genome. Science 352, aae0344 (2016)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2608">45</a></sup> (scaffold N50 of 23.1âMb), <i>V. angularis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Sakai, H. et al. The power of single molecule real-time sequencing technology in the de novo assembly of a eukaryotic genome. Sci. Rep. 5, 16780 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2614">20</a></sup> (scaffold N50 of 3.0âMb), <i>O. thomaeum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="VanBuren, R. et al. Single-molecule sequencing of the desiccation-tolerant grass Oropetium thomaeum. Nature 527, 508â511 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2620">21</a></sup> (contig N50 of 2.4âMb) and <i>Lates calcarifer</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Vij, S. et al. Chromosomal-level assembly of the Asian Seabass genome using long sequence reads and multi-layered scaffolding. PLoS Genet. 12, e1005954 (2016)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2627">46</a></sup> (scaffold N50 of 1.19âMb). A rapid increase in PacBio sequencing for similar large-scale assemblies can be expected in the near future.</p><p>The draft genome has enhanced the understanding of the genetic basis of floricultural traits in <i>I. nil</i>. It was possible to catalogue <i>Tpn1</i> family transposons along with the putative autonomous element, <i>TpnA1</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Data 2</a>). The <i>Tpn1</i> transposons were distributed across all 15 chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 19</a>) and one copy per 126 genes (339 copies per 42,783 genes) was observed. Most of them retain apparently functional <i>cis</i> elements, TIRs and SRRs suggesting that they are capable of transposition. In addition, <i>TpnA1</i>, <i>TpnA2</i>, <i>TpnA3</i> and <i>TpnA4</i> also encode putative transposases (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). These features should be the basis for <i>Tpn1</i> transposons to act as the major mutagen in the mutant cultivars of <i>I. nil.</i> The <i>ct</i> mutation is traditionally called as â<i>uzu</i>â, and the key mutation of the barley green revolution was also named after <i>I. nil</i>âs <i>uzu</i> (<i>contracted</i>) because of their common semi-dwarf phenotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Chono, M. et al. A semidwarf phenotype of barley uzu results from a nucleotide substitution in the gene encoding a putative brassinosteroid receptor. Plant Physiol. 133, 1209â1219 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2697">47</a></sup>. It was also possible to identify the strong candidate for the <i>CT</i> gene by using the combination of the draft genome and classical linkage map, demonstrating the capability of the assembled draft genome. It can be expected that the draft genome will maximize future use of the abundant mutants and genetic knowledge of <i>I. nil</i>. Comparative analysis revealed that each of <i>I. nil</i>, tomato and kiwifruit had independent WGD events in their genomes, even though they all belonged to Asterids. One of the major reasons for the fruit-specific gene neo-functionalization in tomato is reported to be because of a large number of genes triplicated from the recent WGD event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Tomato sequencing consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â641 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2711">38</a></sup>. It could be assumed that the lineage specific WGDs, observed in <i>I. nil</i>, tomato and kiwifruit, could have had a major role in shaping the diverse evolution of these plant species.
