Being the only pseudo-chromosomal level genome assembly in Convolvulaceae, the genome sequence, linkage map and DNA clones developed in this study will facilitate not only future studies on <i>I. nil</i> and its related species, but also aid comparative genomic studies in Solanales.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Plant materials and sequencing</h3><p>An individual of <i>I. nil</i> Tokyo Kokei Standard (TKS) line was propagated clonally and genomic DNA isolated from the flower petals of young buds was used for whole genome sequencing. A 20âkb library (BluePippin size selection at 7âkb) for P5-C3 chemistry was constructed. Ninety SMRT cells were first sequenced on PacBio RS II system. Furthermore, sequencing libraries were prepared using the Illumina TruSeq DNA Sample Prep kit and Nextera Mate Pair Sample Prep kit. Two paired-end and six mate-pair libraries were constructed and sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq2500, with a read length of 150âbp. To validate the accuracy of the reference assembly, end sequencing of a JMHiBa BAC library was carried out using the ABI 3730xl DNA Analyzer. The TKS line was also used for construction of cDNA and BAC libraries for EST sequences. All primers used are listed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 20</a>. The genome size was estimated using a flow cytometer. For transcriptome analysis, tissues from flowers, stems, leaves, and seed coat (maternal tissue) of the individual; embryos and roots of its self-pollinated progeny were used, and the mRNA-Seq libraries were constructed using the Illumina TruSeq mRNASeq Sample Preparation Kit (version 2) from 600âng of total RNA, collected from each of the indicated tissues, according to the manufacturerâs instructions. Sequencing was conducted as paired end reads of 101âbp on Illumina HiSeq2000. Details of the sampling for the transcriptome analysis are shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 21</a>. An F2 hybrid population of <i>I. nil</i> lines TKS Ã Africa (Q63) was used to construct a RAD-tag based linkage map. Two double-digested RAD libraries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Ly, T. et al. Development of EST-SSR markers of Ipomoea nil. Breed. Sci. 62, 99â104 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2745">17</a></sup> were prepared, as described before<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Sakaguchi, S. et al. High-throughput linkage mapping of Australian white cypress pine (Callitris glaucophylla) and map transferability to related species. Tree Genet. Genomes 11, 121 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2750">48</a></sup> with slight modifications of the restriction enzymes and adapters. The restriction enzyme pairs were <i>Nde</i>I/<i>Bgl</i>II and <i>Mse</i>I/<i>Bgl</i>II (New England Biolabs), and the adapter sequences of TruSeq MseI_NdeI and BglII adaptors are listed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 20</a>. The prepared libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq2500 platform as 151-bp single-end reads. Forty-three <i>I. nil</i> lines (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 16</a>) were also used to characterize the <i>CT</i> gene. The <i>a3-flecked</i> mutant, Q1072, was used to isolate <i>TnpA</i> and <i>TnpD</i> mRNA, and an authentic <i>s</i> mutant line, Q721, was used for genetic complementation test for the <i>kbt</i> mutant, Q837.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Genome assembly</h3><p>Before assembling the Illumina short read data set, adapters were trimmed using Cutadapt v1.2.1 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Martin, M. Cutadapt removes adapter sequences from high-throughput sequencing reads. EMBnet J. 17, 10 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2802">49</a>). Using k-mer frequencies of the short insert libraries, SOAPdenovo2âs error correction module (v2.01) was used to correct errors with the following parameters: â-l 80 -Q 33 -j 1 -o 3 -r 50â. The processed reads were assembled, scaffolded and gap-filled using SOAPdenovo2 assembler v2.04 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2805">22</a>) with a k-mer value of 115. The work-flow (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>) of the assembly of longer PacBio reads began with contig assembly using HGAP3 pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Chin, C.-S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods 10, 563â569 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2812">50</a></sup> from SMRTanalysis v2.3.0. For HGAP3, the following parameters were used: PreAssembler Filter v1 (minimum sub-read length=500, minimum polymerase read length=100, and minimum polymerase read quality=0.80); PreAssembler v2 (minimum seed read length=6,000, number of seed read chunks=6, alignment candidates per chunk=10, total alignment candidates=24, minimum coverage for correction=6, and blasr options=âminReadLength=200, maxScore=1,000, maxLCPLength =16, and noSplitSubReadsâ); AssembleUnitig v1 (genome size=750âMb, target coverage=30, overlap error rate=0.06, minimum overlap length=40, and overlapper k-mer=14); Mapping (Maximum number of hits per read=10, maximum divergence %=30, minimum anchor size=12, and pbalign options=âseed =1, minAccuracy=0.75, minLength=50, useQuality, and placeRepeatsRandomlyâ). The polymerase N50 and the sub-read N50 at the assembly phase was recorded as 12.3 and 10.5âkb, respectively. The initial assembly was followed by two rounds of polishing by Quiver. To correct PacBio residual errors, the Illumina reads were aligned against the contigs using BWA v0.7.12 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with BurrowsâWheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2815">51</a>). After sorting the alignments and marking duplicates using Picard tools v2.1.1 ( <a href="http://picard.sourceforge.net/">http://picard.sourceforge.net/</a>), Genome Analysis ToolKit v3.5 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="McKenna, A. et al. The genome analysis toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297â1303 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2826">52</a>) was used to perform local realignment around in-dels and to call variants using the module, HaplotypeCaller. Variant filtering was performed using the expression: âDP&lt;20.0||QD&lt;2.0||FS&gt;60.0||MQ&lt;40.0â. The homozygous in-dels were treated as errors, and were replaced with Illumina read bases in the assembled contigs using FastaAlternateReferenceMaker. MUMmer v3.23 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2829">53</a>) was used to identify and remove contigs, if more than 50% of their sequence was either mitochondrial or chloroplast sequence. Smaller contigs, which had greater than 98% sequence coverage in other contigs with at least 98% sequence identity, were also removed from the assembly. The contigs were then scaffolded with the help of 15 and 20âkb Illumina mate pair read libraries, with the options âno_score and e=10â using BESST scaffolder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Sahlin, K., Vezzi, F., Nystedt, B., Lundeberg, J. &amp; Arvestad, L. BESSTâefficient scaffolding of large fragmented assemblies. BMC Bioinformatics 15, 281 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2833">54</a></sup>. A first round of splitting chimeric scaffolds was performed before gap-filling. PacBio reads were utilized to gap-fill the scaffolds using PBJelly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="English, A. C. et al. Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with Pacific Biosciences RS long-read sequencing technology. PLoS ONE 7, e47768 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2837">55</a></sup> with the blasr options âminMatch=8, minPctIdentity=70, bestn =1, nCandidates=20, maxScore=500, and noSplitSubreadsâ. If the flanking sequences, at the gap junctions, had an overlap of more than 1âkb, those gaps were filled by joining the flanking sequences manually.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Linkage map construction and pseudo-chromosome assignment</h3><p>The RAD-seq technique<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Baird, N. A. et al. Rapid SNP discovery and genetic mapping using sequenced RAD markers. PLoS ONE 3, e3376 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2849">23</a></sup> was employed to sequence 2 parent samples (TKS and Africa lines) and 207 progeny samples. The Illumina short reads from the parent samples and progeny samples were aligned against the assembly using BWA v0.7.12 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with BurrowsâWheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2852">51</a>). The reads which were not tagged as uniquely mapped, and those which did not have the requisite restriction enzyme cut site were filtered out. STACKS v1.37 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Catchen, J. M. et al. Stacks: building and genotyping loci de novo from short-read sequences. G3 1, 171â182 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2855">24</a>) was used to identify SNP and the following two criteria were used to filter markers: (a) Each marker should be present in at least 80% of the samples, and (b) Each sample should have at least 80% of the markers. Also, 150âbp flanking regions from either side of each SNP location was extracted from the assembly and was aligned against each other using BLAST to check for repetitive regions. Any region with an alignment length of longer than 150 bases were filtered out. Onemap<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Margarido, G. R. A., Souza, A. P. &amp; Garcia, A. A. F. OneMap: software for genetic mapping in outcrossing species. Hereditas 144, 78â79 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2859">56</a></sup> was used to create linkage maps with an LOD score of 30. TMAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Cartwright, D. A., Troggio, M., Velasco, R. &amp; Gutin, A. Genetic mapping in the presence of genotyping errors. Genetics 176, 2521â2527 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2863">57</a></sup> was used to re-order the linkage map, along with manual inspection. The original classical map contained 10 linkage groups (LGs), although <i>I. nil</i> has 15 chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Yasui, K. Studies on Pharbitis nil Chois. II Chromosome number. Bot. Mag. Tokyo 42, 480â485 (1928)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2871">15</a></sup>. The marker genes from seven of the 10 LGs of the classical map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Hagiwara, T. Genes and chromosome maps in the Japanese morning glory. Bull. Res. Coll. Agr. Vet. Sci. Nihon Univ. 5, 34â56 (1956)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2875">16</a></sup> were mapped in the current RAD-based linkage maps, and the LGs were named 1 to 6 and 10 correspondingly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 22</a>). Because two LGs in our RAD-marker based map corresponded to LG3 in the classical map, they were accordingly assigned as LG3 and LG11 with the corresponding marker genes being <i>DUSKY</i> and <i>SPECKLED</i> respectively. This coincided with the fact that the <i>DUSKY</i> and <i>SPECKLED</i> genes were mapped on the different linkage groups in the older linkage analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Imai, Y. Linkage groups of the Japanese morning glory. Genetics 14, 223â255 (1929)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2895">30</a></sup>. LGs 7 to 9 and 12 to 15 were numbered randomly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Mis-assembly elimination and assembly validation</h3><p>Before anchoring scaffolds to pseudo-chromosomes, chimeric assemblies were first resolved using linkage maps and BAC-end sequences. Contigs were first aligned against the scaffolds using the NUCmer module within MUMmer v3.23 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2906">53</a>) to identify the contig locations in the scaffolds. If a scaffold contained a stretch of linkage markers pointing to two different linkage groups with a scaffold junction (N) in between, it was considered a chimera and was split into two at the junction. If the mis-assembly occurred at the contig level, the bac-end alignments were used as a key in splitting chimeric contigs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Methods</a>). Based on the order of the linkage maps, the scaffolds were merged using Ns as gaps to form pseudo-chromosomes. The orientations of the scaffolds were determined using the marker order, and the orientations of scaffolds with inadequate markers were ignored but included as part of the pseudo-chromosomes. The circular view of the genome was generated using Circos<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Krzywinski, M. et al. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 19, 1639â1645 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2913">58</a></sup>. CEGMA v2.5 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061â1067 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2916">26</a>) and BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2920">27</a></sup>, two commonly used genome assembly validation pipelines, were used to validate the completeness of genes in the assembly. BLAT was used to align ESTs and BAC-end paired reads against the assembly. In-house scripts were written, which calculated paired BLAT scores from both the BAC-end read pairs and picked up the best paired hits based on the combined score. RNA-seq reads were trimmed using Trimmomatic v0.33 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114â2120 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2924">59</a>) and TopHat v2.1.0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Kim, D. et al. TopHat2: accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol. 14, R36 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2927">60</a>) was used to align the RNA-seq reads with default parameters. Tandem repeats finder v4.07b (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573â580 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2930">61</a>) was used to identify tandem repeats using the parameters â1 1 2 80 5 200 2000 -d -hâ. Inspection of short tandem repeats at the ends of the contigs revealed the monomer âAAACCCTâ to be the telomeric repeat. Manual inspection of the tandem repeats also revealed the centromeric repeat monomer to be of approximately 173âbp in length (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). A tetramer centromeric repeat sequence was used to search against the whole output of tandem repeats finder using BLAST. The BLAST alignment results were screened for monomer sequences closer to 173âbp length to identify centromeric repeat candidates. Tandem centromeric repeat stretches (&gt;3âkb) were merged, when they were within a distance of 50âkb and the longest stretch for every chromosome was identified to approximate the putative position of the centromeres. Infernal v1.1.1 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Nawrocki, E. P. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.1: 100-fold faster RNA homology searches. Bioinformatics 29, 2933â2935 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2936">62</a>) was used to identify rDNA clusters by searching against Rfam v12.0.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Repeat analysis and gene prediction</h3><p><i>De novo</i> repeat identification was done using RepeatModeler v1.0.7 which combines RECON and RepeatScout<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21, i351âi358 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d3400e2951">63</a></sup> programs, followed by RepeatMasker v4.0.2 to achieve the final results. <i>Tpn1</i> family transposons were detected using the following approach: The TIRs of the <i>Tpn1</i> transposons (28âbp in length) were searched using BLAST; the aligned TIR co-ordinates were sorted by their locations; if two nearby TIRs contained the same TSDs (3â5âbp), they were nominated as <i>Tpn1</i> family elements. The sub-terminal repeats were also identified using BLAST to determine the orientation of the <i>Tpn1</i> elements. A translated BLAST search against the identified transposons using TnpA and TnpD sequences from maize and snapdragon as queries revealed non-autonomous <i>TpnA3</i> and <i>TpnA4</i>. To isolate autonomous <i>Tpn1</i> transposons, the cDNA fragments of <i>TnpA</i> and <i>TnpD</i> homologue were isolated from Q1072 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Methods</a>). Using the isolated cDNA sequences as query, <i>TpnA1</i> and <i>TpnA2</i> were identified by screening against the assembled scaffolds using BLAST. As the 5â² terminal of <i>TpnA1</i> was not assembled completely in the genome sequence, a BAC clone from TKS carrying the entire <i>TpnA1</i> sequence was isolated and characterized (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Methods</a>). Repeats obtained by both the above mentioned approaches were masked for gene prediction. The genes harbouring <i>Tpn1</i> transposon insertions were identified using the gene and the transposon co-ordinates and were annotated using the web version of BLASTX. Gene models were predicted using Augustus v3.2.2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics 19, ii215âii225 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d3400e3005">29</a>) with tomato as the reference species, using hints from RNA-seq alignments, and also allowing prediction of untranslated regions (UTRs). Because of the scarcity of complete CDs of <i>I. nil</i> in public databases, independently, Augustus was also used to predict gene models, after training using CEGMA predicted genes, and the procedure resulted in more than 55,000 gene models. The 189 complete CDs sequences already available in NCBI were downloaded and compared against the predicted gene models using BLAT. Tomato based gene models showed that 116 out of 189 CDs were perfectly complete, whereas CEGMA trained gene models showed that only 61 out of 189 CDs were complete and hence, the tomato based gene predictions were used for further analysis. The gene models were translated to proteins and were aligned against proteins from UniProt-Swiss-Prot and UniProt-TrEMBL databases using NCBI BLAST+ v2.2.29 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403â410 (1990)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d3400e3011">64</a>). Using an e-value cut-off of e-5 for annotation, alignments from the Swiss-Prot database were given preference ahead of the TrEMBL database. In other words, TrEMBL annotations were assigned for only those entries without a Swiss-Prot annotation. To extract protein domain annotations, InterProScan v5.19-58.0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236â1240 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d3400e3014">65</a>) was used to assign Pfam domains to the gene models. Gene Ontology (GO) terms were extracted from the Pfam annotations as well as UniProt annotations.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Characterization of the <i>CT</i> gene</h3><p>The genomic fragments of the <i>CT</i> gene were amplified by PCR. The primers used are listed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 20</a>, and KOD FX Neo polymerase (TOYOBO) was used following the manufacturerâs instructions. PCR fragments were purified using QIAquick PCR purification kit (QIAGEN) and were directly sequenced with the ABI Prism 3100 Genetic Analyzer and BigDye version 3.1 chemistry (Applied Biosystems). The entire genomic <i>CT</i> gene with the <i>Tpn1</i> transposons in the six lines including Q220 (<i>ct-1</i>), Q787 (<i>ct-1</i>), Q851 (<i>ct-2</i>), Q853 (<i>ct-3</i>), Q594 (<i>ct-w</i>) and Q848 (<i>ct-w</i>) were analysed. The transposon insertion sites in the other 13 mutants listed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 16</a> were also analysed. First strand cDNA was synthesized using ReverTra Ace qPCR RT Master Mix with gDNA Remover (TOYOBO), and cDNA fragments were amplified with KOD FX Neo using InCYP90-Fw1 and InCYP90-Rv1 primers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Table 20</a>). Normal expression of another <i>CT</i> candidate gene of INIL05g28523 encoding CYP85A2 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>) located more than 5.2âcM far from <i>A3</i> was confirmed by RTâPCR and sequence analyses (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 16</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Comparative analysis</h3><p>Protein sequences were downloaded from tomato, potato, capsicum, grape and rice. OrthoMCL v2.0.9 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d3400e3087">41</a>) was used to construct orthologous gene families, with an inflation parameter of 1.5. Before OrthoMCL, an all-vs-all BLAST was performed to find similar matches from different species, and the BLAST results were filtered with an e-value cut-off of e-5, a minimum alignment length of 50âbp, and a percentage match cut-off of 50. AgriGO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Du, Z., Zhou, X., Ling, Y., Zhang, Z. &amp; Su, Z. agriGO: a GO analysis toolkit for the agricultural community. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, w64âw70 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d3400e3091">66</a></sup> was used for finding GO enrichments in <i>I. nil</i> specific gene families. MAFFT v7.221 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Katoh, K., Misawa, K., Kuma, K. &amp; Miyata, T. MAFFT: a novel method for rapid multiple sequence alignment based on fast Fourier transform. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 3059â3066 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d3400e3097">67</a>) was used for multiple sequence alignments of the resultant single copy orthologs, and trimAl v1.4 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Capella-GutiÃ©rrez, S., Silla-MartÃ­nez, J. M. &amp; GabaldÃ³n, T. trimAl: a tool for automated alignment trimming in large-scale phylogenetic analyses. Bioinformatics 25, 1972â1973 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d3400e3100">68</a>) was used to remove poorly aligned regions and to back-translate protein alignments to CDs alignments. The alignments were filtered using the criteria that coding sequences from each of the species should have covered at least 95% of the multiple sequence alignments, thereby, reducing the gaps to less than 5% of the alignments. RAxML v8.2.4 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312â1313 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d3400e3104">42</a>) was used to build Maximum Likelihood phylogenetic trees using the GTRGAMMA model, with rice as an out-group. BEAST v2.3.1 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Bouckaert, R. et al. BEAST 2: a software platform for Bayesian evolutionary analysis. PLoS Comput. Biol. 10, 1â6 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d3400e3107">43</a>) was used to estimate the divergence times with the Jules Cantor substitution model, with a log normal relaxed clock and Yule model. The chain length of MCMC analysis was 10,000,000. TimeTree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Hedges, S. B., Marin, J., Suleski, M., Paymer, M. &amp; Kumar, S. Tree of life reveals clock-like speciation and diversification. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 832â845 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d3400e3111">44</a></sup> is a public database containing divergence time estimates from various publications along with their own estimation. These estimates, ignoring the outliers, were used for selecting the range of lower and upper uniform calibration priors. The lower and upper calibration values were chosen as 1.9â12.8, 15.6â41, 58.6â95.1, 93.3â128.3, 101.2â156.3 and 110â216 for the most common ancestor of the seven species belonging to <i>Solanum</i>, Solanaceae, Solanales, asterids, dicotyledons and all plants, respectively. FigTree ( <a href="http://tree.bio.ed.ac.uk/software/figtree">http://tree.bio.ed.ac.uk/software/figtree</a>) was used to view the phylogenetic trees. Synteny analysis of the 15 pseudo-chromosomes against the chromosomes of other species was performed using the MCScanX toolkit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d3400e3125">69</a></sup> utilizing the following options: â-m 15 -e 1e-10 -k 50â. PAMLâs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms13295#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d3400e3130">70</a></sup> yn00 module was used to calculate the Ks values of the orthologous and paralogous gene pairs in the sytenic regions using Nei-Gojobori method. The assembled genome was compared against the genome of <i>I. trifida</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms13295#MOESM996">Supplementary Fig. 19</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Data availability</h3><p>All sequencing data used in this work are available from the DNA DataBank of Japan (DDBJ) Sequence Read Archive (DRA) under the accession numbers DRA001121, DRA002710, and DRA004158 for PacBio and Illumina sequencing, DRA002647 for RNA-seq, and DRA002758 for RAD-seq. The genomic assembly sequences are available from accession numbers BDFN01000001-BDFN01003416 (scaffolds), and two organelle DNA sequences are available from accession numbers AP017303-AP017304. The EST and BAC-end sequences are available from accession numbers HY917605-HY949060 and GA933005-GA974698, respectively. Accession numbers for the <i>CONTRACTED</i> gene, its mutant alleles, and <i>Tpn1</i> family elements are LC101804-LC101815. All the above data has been released for public access, as of August 31, 2016, and the accessibility has been verified by the authors.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec20" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec20-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec20">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec20-content"><p><b>How to cite this article:</b> Hoshino, A. <i>et al</i>. Genome sequence and analysis of the Japanese morning glory <i>Ipomoea nil</i>. <i>Nat. Commun.</i> <b>7,</b> 13295 doi: 10.1038/ncomms13295 (2016).</p><p><b>Publisher's note:</b> Springer Nature remains neutral with regard to jurisdictional claims in published maps and institutional affiliations.</p></div></div></section>

Sakakibara and E.N. designed the project. A.H. and E.N. prepared plants and isolated nucleic acids. V.J. and Y. Sakakibara assembled the genome sequence. A.T., H. Noguchi, Y. Minakuchi, Y. Morita and A.F. performed genome and BAC sequencing. A.H., V.J., E.N., A.T., H.K.N., T.I. and Y. Sakakibara analysed the draft genome sequence. A.J.N., M.Y., M.N.H. and H.K. constructed RAD-seq library. A.H., M.S., A.K., T.S., P.C., E.A., S. Tabata, Y.H., K.S. and K.P. prepared the EST libraries. A.H., H. Nishide, I.U., Y.T. and S.I. analysed the EST sequences. A.H., S. Tanaka, T.S. and Y. Kohara performed cDNA sequencing. A.H., E.N., K.Y. and V.J. analysed the transposons. A.H. analysed the <i>CONTRACTED</i> gene. Y. Suzuki and S.S. performed RNA-seq. A.H., V.J., Y. Koda and Y. Sakakibara analysed RNA-seq data. V.J. performed comparative analysis, gene prediction, and functional annotation. A.H., Y. Sakakibara, E.N. and V.J. wrote the manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to

    </section>
